  <meta content="Here in Boulder we get magnificent sunsets, especially in the summer when the clouds interplay with the mountains to the west. But I have never seen anything like this: the shadow of Washington states Mt. Rainier cast along the clouds at sunrise: Holy (yes, in this case appropriately) Haleakala! [Click to cascadenate.] Thats amazing. Mt. " property="og:description"/>

        <div class="gridColumn six">
         <div class="contentWell">
          <div id="blog-header">
           <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/" style="display:block;width:100%;height:100%;">
            <div class="avatar">
             <img alt="" src="	

http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/wp-content/themes/discoverblog/images/blog_icons/large/plait.png"/>
            </div>
            <div class="words">
             <h1>
              Bad Astronomy
             </h1>
             <p class="credit">
             </p>
            </div>
           </a>
          </div>
          <div class="singlepost">
           <div class="post" id="post-main-39903">
            <div class="entry">
             <div class="navigation">
              <span class="prev">
               
               <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2011/10/26/ask-me-something-on-slashdot/" rel="prev">
                Ask me something on Slashdot!
               </a>
              </span>
              <span class="next">
               <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2011/10/27/a-cosmic-halloween-gallery-things-that-go-boo-in-the-night/" rel="next">
                A cosmic Halloween gallery: things that go BOO in the night
               </a>
               
              </span>
             </div>
             <h1 class="post-title single">
              <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2011/10/26/the-long-shadow-of-mt-rainier/" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to The long shadow of Mt. Rainier">
               The long shadow of Mt. Rainier
              </a>
             </h1>
             <div class="meta">
              <span class="meta-date">
               By
               <a href="http://discovermagazine.com/authors?name=Phil+Plait" style="color:#555">
                Phil Plait
               </a>
               | 
		October 26, 2011 12:49 pm
              </span>
             </div>
             <div class="shareIcons" style="border-top:1px solid #999;border-bottom:1px solid #999;padding:7px 0 7px 0;margin-bottom:20px;">
              <div class="addthis_toolbox addthis_default_style addthis_32x32_style">
               <a class="addthis_button_preferred_1">
               </a>
               <a class="addthis_button_preferred_2">
               </a>
               <a class="addthis_button_preferred_3">
               </a>
               <a class="addthis_button_preferred_4">
               </a>
               <a class="addthis_button_compact">
               </a>
               <a class="addthis_counter addthis_bubble_style">
               </a>
              </div>
             </div>
             <p>
              Here in Boulder we get magnificent sunsets, especially in the summer when the clouds interplay with the mountains to the west. But I have
              <em>
               never
              </em>
              seen
              <em>
               anything
              </em>
              like
              <a href="http://www.komonews.com/weather/blogs/scott/132629943.html" target="_blank">
               this
              </a>
              : the shadow of Washington states Mt. Rainier cast along the clouds at sunrise:
             </p>
             <p>
              <a href="http://media.komonews.com/images/P1010901-1.JPG" target="_blank">
               <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-39904" height="343" src="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/files/2011/10/mtrainiershadow.jpg" width="610"/>
              </a>
             </p>
             <p>
              Holy (yes, in this case appropriately) Haleakala! [Click to cascadenate.]
             </p>
             <p>
              Thats
              <em>
               amazing
              </em>
              . Mt. Rainier is a volcano, climbing to a height of over 14,000 feet (4300 meters). There are no other mountains anywhere near that height nearby, so its really prominent in the landscape (by comparison, there are several fourteeners, as theyre called, in the Rockies, so they dont stick out as much though theyre still breathtaking). The rising Sun catches the peak, and the shadow is cast on the underside of the cloud layer. The dramatic
              <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2011/05/03/forget-the-green-lantern-heres-the-green-flash/" target="_blank">
               sunrise colors
              </a>
              really make this an incredibly beautiful shot.
             </p>
             <p>
              The KOMO news site
              <a href="http://www.komonews.com/weather/blogs/scott/132629943.html" target="_blank">
               has lots more pictures of this
              </a>
              , too. Go take a look!
             </p>
             <p>
              And remember, when youre outside, it always pays to look around you for a moment. You never know what incredible vista nature may have in store for you.
             </p>
             <p>
              <em>
               Tip o the snow cap (har har) to
               <a href="http://twitter.com/#!/johnbaxter/status/129260982068445184" target="_blank">
                John Baxter
               </a>
               .
              </em>
             </p>
             <p>
             </p>
             <hr align="left" width="30%"/>
             <font size="-1">
              <em>
               <p>
                Related posts:
               </p>

                 Amazing video of a bizarre, twisting, dancing cloud
                </a>
                <br/>

                 The fist of an angry cloud
                </a>
                <br/>
                
                <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2010/06/17/time-spent-doing-what-you-love-is-never-wasted/" target="_blank">
                 Time spent doing what you love is never wasted
                </a>
                <br/>

                </a>

                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    Tahoma (or Tahomasort of a southern German ch) gave its name to the city of Tacoma. Then we Eurpeans took away its name and called it Mt. Rainier (which gave its name to a brewery).
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    More about the mountain:
                    <br/>
                    <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mt_Rainier#Naming_controversy" rel="nofollow">
                     http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mt_Rainier#Naming_controversy
                    </a>
                    <br/>
                    (and plenty of other sites).
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Mt. Rainier also does amazing lenticular cloud displays.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    John

               <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-312795">
                <div class="commentwrap clearfix">
                 <div class="commentBox clearfix" id="comment-312795">
                  <div class="comment-author vcard">
                   <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-84 photo" height="84" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/5f3d1ff660040377b17d2742b4104768?s=84&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F1.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D84&amp;r=G" width="84"/>
                   <cite class="fn">
                    NickBob
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    John Baxter, Id agree with you more often than not on the primacy of Salish naming, but  Rainy-er is too much of an aptonym for our corner of the country. Besides, most often the pile is simply called The Mountain, despite having plenty of spectacular competition in 2 ranges on the skyline.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Trebuchet, never mind eruptions. Google Rainier mudflows and read about the history of destructive lahars which are a function of ice, earth, and sulphuric acid from the volcano, sleeping or not. The foothills around the park have developed quite a bit over the last 30 years, the Romans at Pompeii were no less foolish. Even Tacoma and Seattle have dirt in some areas that was washed off the slopes in times passed. Japans got nothing on us with geologic peril.
                   </p>
                   <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
                    <span class="commentdate">
                     <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2011/10/26/the-long-shadow-of-mt-rainier/#comment-312795">
                      October 26, 2011 at 6:20 pm
                     </a>
                    </span>
                    <span class="reply">
                    </span>
                   </div>
                  </div>
                 </div>
                </div>

                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    When they were alive, my parents lived in Victoria, B.C., and the test of a really clear day was to be able to see Mount Rainier way off to the southeast, from a hilltop (Mount Tolmie) in Victoria. There it would stand, all alone, as a distant ghostly eminence above the far-off horizon.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    As the crow flies, Seattle is 70 miles southeast from Victoria, and Mount Rainier is an additional 60 miles further away, for a total distance of 130 miles ( 210 kilometers)!
                   </p>

                   </p>

                   <p>
                    If you want to forecast the weather, step outside and look in the direction of Mt. Rainier.
                    <br/>
                    If you can see the peak, its going to rain.
                    <br/>
                    If you cant see the peak, its raining.
                    <br/>
                    TeaPot562
                   </p>

                   <cite class="fn">
                    Znachki
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    Heading south from Seattle, there is a bend in the road where the Mountain appears right in front of you. A vistor asked me once, do you ever get used to that? Ive lived here my whole life, and even worked at Paradise, of course I said, nope  not ever. The shadow happens every year, the real trick is having the weather play along. And this time it played as well as Ive EVER seen it. Sigh
                   </p>
                   <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">

                 <div class="commentBox clearfix" id="comment-312830">
                  <div class="comment-author vcard">
                   <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-84 photo" height="84" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/026c23f442816222c4faf31cea8277f4?s=84&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F0.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D84&amp;r=G" width="84"/>
                   <cite class="fn">
                    Dawn
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    Saw this approx 735a this morning, sliding down Hwy 119 on way to Hwy 101first through the trees (Hoodsport area).
                    <br/>
                    When it is clear, I can usually see Mt Rainier about
                    <br/>
                    1/3 the way down the hwy.  Today, I first  noticed the open horizon &amp; the bank of clouds above &amp; the spectacular color thinking the old red sky at morning, sailor
                    <br/>
                    take warning.  At the next dip in the road, I caught the shadow of the mountain
                    <br/>
                    on the bottom of the clouds before I saw the mountain itself. knew this was probably a once in a lifetime vision..
                   </p>
                   <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">

           /* &lt;![CDATA[ */
		(function() {
			var dropdown = document.getElementById('discoverblog_categories');
			function onCatChange() {
				if ( dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value &gt; 0 ) {
					location.href = "http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/?cat="+dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value;
				}
			}
			dropdown.onchange = onCatChange;
		})();

		(function() {
			var dropdown = document.getElementById('discoverblog_tags');
			function onCatChange() {
				if (dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value) {
					location.href = dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value;
				}
			}
			dropdown.onchange = onCatChange;
		})();

		(function() {
			var dropdown = document.getElementById('discoverblog_archives');
			function onCatChange() {
				if (dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value) {
					location.href = dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value;
				}
			}
			dropdown.onchange = onCatChange;
		})();
